*Shinzo Abe* remained unrepentant towards the atrocities committed by the Japanese army during the Second World War especially the massacre at Nanking. He was unapologetic to China, and also angered all his ASEAN neighbours by persistently insisting on honouring the Japanese war criminals enshrined in the Yasukuni Shrine.   *Nobusuke Kishi* (13 November 1896 - 7 August 1987), a Class A war criminal in the war of aggression against China, a Japanese right wing known for his exploitative rule of the Japanese puppet state of Manchokuo in Northeast China in the 1930s and was nicknamed the "Monster of the ShÅwa era" (æ˜­å’Œã®å¦–æ€ª; ShÅwa no yÅkai).   Kishi was notorious of his brutality when he served as Manchukuo's Deputy Minister of Industrial Development. Kishi felt the Chinese were more akin to dogs than human beings and would only understand brute force, that Chinese were merely _'mechanical instruments of the Imperial Army, non-human automatons that should absolutely be obedient'_ to their Japanese masters.   With the US support he became the Prime Minister of Japan (1957-1960). As prime minister, Kishi's mishandling of the 1960 revision of the US-Japan Security Treaty _(the treaty that allows the United States to maintain military bases on Japanese soil),_ led to the massive 1960 Anpo protests which were the largest protests in Japan's modern history and which forced him to resign in disgrace.   On 14 July 1960, Kishi was attacked by a knife-wielding assailant as he was leaving the prime minister's residence to host a garden party celebrating Hayato Ikeda's impending ascension to the premiership. For the rest of his life, Kishi remained devoted to the cause of revising the Japanese Constitution to get rid of *_Article 9_* and remilitarizing Japan.  On 7 August 1987, Nobusuke Kishi died after months of illness. Shinzo Abe is his grandson, who is also an extreme right wing antagonist against China.  Just like his belligerent grandfather, Shinzo Abe sought to eliminate *_Article 9_* which states: *_'Aspiring sincerely to an international peace based on justice and order, the Japanese people forever renounce war as a sovereign right of the nation and the threat or use of force as means of settling international disputes.'_*

It happened in Yamato-Saidaiji, a suburb of Nara City.  I lived in Saidaiji for 12 years.  You can even see the first apartment building I lived in in some of the shots.  My kids were born a block from there and the first school I taught in was across the street.  It was right in front of the Kintetsu Railways station that was my home station.  The Starbucks you see in the background of many of the pictures was one of the places my friends and I would often meet and whenever I walked by (several times a day) I'd always look to see which of my friends was there.  Seeing something this infamous happen in what used to be home to me is mind blowing.  My heart goes out to my friends and former neighbors because this must be truly shattering for them.  Japanese culture is collectivist rather than individualistic like many western societies, so it can be difficult for westerners to fathom the sense of collective guilt a Japanese community feels when one of it's members commits an atrocity of this magnitude, even if it seems unwarranted.  Most Japanese nationals feel a sense of pride, and rightly so, for having one of the safest societies on the planet.    This happening in Nara is a tragic irony.  Abe Shinzo was the first stable prime minister after a period of instability at the top level of the national government.  Nara was the first permanent capitol of Japan.  You can actually see one of the reconstructed buildings from the Heijokyo palace site at the end of the street where Mr. Abe was shot.  One of the reasons the capitol moved around in antiquity was before the arrival of Buddhism, people felt that a place was spiritually tainted if someone died there, and the more influential the person was, the darker the shadow.  Nara was the first place Buddhism really took off in Japan and when the capitol finally did move to Heiankyo (Kyoto) it was more for separating religion and government.  They wanted to move the imperial court farther away from the influence of some of the larger religious institutions in Nara like Todaiji temple.    I'm sorry for writing a book in the comments.  I guess I'm still trying to get my head around it and this is one of the ways I'm dealing with it.  My kids grew up mostly during his tenure and his face is what pops into their minds when they hear the phrase 'prime minister.'  I still have to go there semi-regularly so my kids can visit their family there.  I know when I inevitably pass that spot, I'll feel a hollow icy chill followed by a sense of reverence for Abe-san and wonder why fate so often takes away the best among us prematurely.

Didn't agree with many of his views, but it's unquestionable how much of a statesman he was... irrespective of his own political inclinations and the views of his party, it seemed to me he always managed to keep balance between the extremes and focus on problems and worries that affected most of the Japanese population instead of playing favoritism and ruling only with particular interests in mind. Japanese economy may have been in recession over his entire time there, but there are lots of positive changes that happened in Japanese society that most of international media coverage does not recognize because it's not in their interest. You know how you hear about suicide in Japan all the time? It has been reversed to a point that most western nations have worse rates these days, did you know that? There is a bunch of stigmatized "only in Japan" problems often reinforced and exploited without context by foreign view "documentaries" that are not huge problems there anymore thanks to the hard and often hidden work of politicians there. I understand and agree with people who questions things such as historical revisionism, militarism, revising the constitution, among a few other controversial topics, but we need to understand that while he was prime minister there was no radical action to push these far over the will of people. There was a balance kept between the wish of people on all sides of the spectrum, which is what great leaders do. It's not only this politician or that party that has these radical views too, it's a portion of the population. Keeping the most extreme views at bay is what good politicians do. It was a shock when he left office due to health issues, this news is just surreal. Political views for political views, he was far from acting in a radical manner in any way, so it all just doesn't make much sense. There are far more radical politicians in Japan's governmental system. Feels like this was done much more on attention rather than politics disagreements. Regardless, this shouldn't happen in any democracy, and much less a peaceful country like Japan... the damage extends far beyond one man's life, career and legacy - it could change the entire dynamics of politics in Japan. The nation does not need political radicalism, no country does. May he rest in peace, his family be comforted by his achievements, and that his legacy is seen as a way to keep peace, moderation and diplomacy.

Why am I seeing so many news stories express shock at the fact that this happened in a country where it's so difficult to get a gun, when it was known from the very beginning (because the police recovered the weapon) that the assailant used a HOMEMADE gun in this shooting? As in, a gun crafted from segments of pipe and other miscellaneous parts?  That's a pretty major part of this story, I feel, as it shows that Japan's gun laws did NOT fail Abe-san -- rather, that his assailant went to great lengths to pull this off, and there was really basically nothing that could've been done to prevent it.  I mean, this particular news story is about Abe-san himself, and his death is absolutely a tragedy, and a huge shock to the entire nation (and the world, really). But it almost seemed as though the newscaster here didn't even KNOW that a makeshift gun was used in the crime, and that's got me wondering how many news outlets simply aren't reporting that fact...

Let's set things straight. Abe was not an uncontroversial figure. Personally, I have always, strongly disapproved of his right wing nationalistic rhetoric, strongly militaristic policies, numerous denials of Japanese war crimes, and countless visits to shrines that honor men who murder-raped little girls and tore the eyes out of alive children's skulls. Political assassinations are very rare in Japan, but when you have that kind of personal history, it is not that surprising that someone would try to take your life. Even so, the man was a law abiding citizen with a family and a loving wife. As much as I disagree with many of his policies and views, I strongly feel that the man, overall, did what he thought was best for his country. No one deserves to get shot like that followed by hours of stress, excruciating pain, and a needless death.  R.I.P. Abe Shinzou.  (Some of you may think my comment is unnecessarily cruel or insensitive, but I think it is important to be candid about these things at times like this. Everyone has bad and good sides, and you need to remember the good with the bad.)

Condolences to Japan Ex PM shocking news
The way Abe was attacked is not unusual, even though gun violence involving average people is rare. In 1990 Nagasaki Mayor Motoshima was shot in the back. His successor was also shot twice in the back but did not survive. Both attacks were connected to right-wing/yakuza. Let's see if Abe's killer is one of these types. In late 1988 during his third mayoral term, news that the ShÅwa Emperor was gravely ill and not expected to live long had put Japan into a somber mood. On December 7 Motoshima was asked in a city council meeting by a council member from the Communist Party his opinion of the Emperor's responsibility for World War II. He answered: Forty-three years have passed since the end of the war, and I think we have had enough chance to reflect on the nature of the war. From reading various accounts from abroad and having been a soldier myself, involved in military education, I do believe that the emperor bore responsibility for the war... â€” (Buruma 1994:249) The Liberal Democratic Party Prefectural Committee immediately demanded that he retract the statement, but Motoshima rejected the demand, saying he could not betray his conscience. In response, the Party Prefectural Committee removed him from the post of committee advisor, and many conservative organizations vilified the mayor. Furthermore, many extreme right-wing groups converged on Nagasaki and demonstrated in the streets with more than eighty speaker trucks calling out for divine retribution upon the mayor. On January 18, 1990, when police had relaxed their guard on the mayor, a member of the right-wing group Seikijuku shot Motoshima in the back, but he survived the assassination attempt.

This is terrible news. To my understanding he was a much respected and much loved man, with moderate positions while serving a liberal democracy. He was perhaps the most influential leader of Japan since the end of WW2. While the report claims "the left" didn't like his policies, their interpretation of left/right politics is vastly different to what you might see in a fanatical country like the USA. Even "conservatives" in much of the modern world still understand the importance of liberal freedoms, science, medicine, public health and education etc. A Conservative in Japan isn't very dissimilar to a Liberal in Canada. No doubt we'll learn more in the coming days and weeks, but obviously political violence is extremely rare in Japan. This is why my instinct is to consider that this might be a mental health issue. Mental health services in Japan are considerably lacking, there's a preference to ignore such issues and very little funding is provided. It's considered "shameful" for someone to experience mental health concerns and this leads to very high suicide rates. It's speculation, of course, but in a country like Japan it's incredibly unlikely that this was political extremism, unless it was facilitated from outside the country. I hope they get to the truth soon. May he rest in peace.
